B003BVK36Q|4.0|John Le Carre is one of our most fluent and literate writers, writing with simple elegance and poignant wisdom.  He earned world-wide fame with \\"The Spy Who Came Out of the Cold,\\" perhaps his finest work, and he pushed himself to his suspenseful best with \\"The Little Drummer Girl.\\"  His British spy novels prove he is our most perceptive chronicler of the Cold War, and like many \\"Cold War Warriors\\" he suddenly found himself with a profession but without a cause after the collapse of the Soviet Union.  He whole-heartedly used his awesome fame and immense literary talent to tackle the splintered, chaotic, and frightening amoral world that emerged after the fall of the Berlin Wall, and while that's praise-worthy what is not is his half-hearted attempts to try to forge a new sense, orderliness, and morality with his new writing.  This was most obvious in \\"The Constant Gardener,\\" a tirade against the global pharmaceutical industry masquerading as a detective novel.  His most recent novel \\"A Most Wanted Man\\" re-writes \\"The Constant Gardener\\" but without the outrageous self-righteousness, and it is the better of his post-Cold War novels.      In \\"The Constant Gardener\\" the heroes (a diplomat's crusading wife and her doctor ally) die before the start of the novel, and the would-be heroes (two young detectives) are relegated to bureaucratic nothingness right away, leaving the most reluctant hero.  In \\"A Most Wanted Man\\" this constellation of characters is re-born but the import of their roles has been re-configured.  A young crusading lawyer now takes center-stage alongside her naive Chechen client.  Two German detectives are their accomplices in trying to, if not make the world a better place, at least try to make sense of it.  The reluctant hero, international financier Tommy Brue, is more in the background, where his conscience languishes and anguishes.  The natural villains of the book (a Russian warlord and his British financier) are long dead, and it is the Americans who take their role.  The plot of the book is obvious from the start, and the ending always assured.  But Le Carre is such a fluent writer and he presents such sympathetic characters that we do follow the much too long book until the very end.     \\"A Most Wanted Man\\" may be Le Carre's last book, and if that is the case it is not a bad way to end a brilliant and startling career.

0375757295|5.0|In Jane Austen's time, young women were taught that it was practically their duty to \\"marry well\\" -- someone of at least equal social/financial standing.<br /><br />But if a woman turned down a suitor for being poor, she ran the risk of losing the man she loved. That's the problem for Anne Elliott, the heroine of Jane Austen's final novel \\"Persuasion\\" -- a delicate romance that takes place AFTER the romance, rejection and heartrending sorrow. There's some slight roughness around the edges, but the story and the characters are simply brilliant.<br /><br />Eight years ago, Anne Elliott was engaged to the handsome, intelligent and impoverished sailor Frederick Wentworth, but was persuaded to dump him by the family friend Lady Russell.<br /><br />Now she's twenty-seven (ancient by the time's standards), and her vain father Sir Walter is facing financial ruin. So he decides to relocate to Bath and rent out the vast family estate -- and it turns out that the new tenant is Frederick's brother-in-law. Of course, Anne still loves Frederick, but he doesn't seem to feel the same, especially since he's rumored to be interested in some younger, flirtier girls.<br /><br />And Anne's worries increase when she joins her family in Bath, where her father is attempting to live the lifestyle he feels he deserves (since he's a baronet). His heir, William Elliott, recently reestablished contact with his relatives -- and he seems very interested in Anne. But Anne suspects that he has ulterior motives... even if she doesn't realize how Frederick truly feels about her.<br /><br />It's pretty obvious that Jane Austen wrote \\"Persuasion\\" late in her life -- not only is Anne Elliott older than her other heroines, but she seems to have been more sympathetic to women who bowed to society's \\"persuasions.\\" This was the last book that Austen wrote before her untimely death, and it was only published posthumously.<br /><br />As a result, the book can be a little rough and the story is rather simple. But Austen's writing is still intense and powerfully vivid. Her prose is elegant and smooth, and her dialogue is full of hidden facets. The half-hidden love story of Anne and Frederick is among Austen's most skillful writing (\\"I am half agony, half hope. Tell me not that I am too late, that such precious feelings are gone for ever\\"), and it's virtually impossible not to be moved by it.<br /><br />And Austen went out of her way to praise the self-made man, who got ahead through merit instead of birth (something that bugs Sir Walter). She also pokes holes in social climbers, vain aristocrats (\\"Few women could think more of their personal appearance than he did\\"), nasty family and false friends.<br /><br />Anne herself is a very rare heroine, both then and now -- she's past her designated \\"marriage\\" years and would have been considered a lost cause. But she remains remains kind, thoughtful, quiet, intelligent, and as time goes on she starts to appreciate her own judgement instead of being \\"persuaded.\\" And Captain Wentworth is a vibrant portrayal of a strong man who worked his way to the top, but had to do so without the woman he loved.<br /><br />Jane Austen's last finished novel is a little rough in places, but the exquisite beauty of Frederick and Anne's love story is simply staggering. Truly a masterpiece.powerfully vivid. Her prose is elegant and smooth, and her dialogue is full of hidden facets. The half-hidden love story of Anne and Frederick is among Austen's most skillful writing (\\"I am half agony, half hope. Tell me not that I am too late, that such precious feelings are gone for ever\\"), and it's virtually impossible not to be moved by it. Is it a conspiracy to get us to listen to their schlock, as religious fanatics are usually the ones who will do anything to try to get you to listen to their plea to worship their Lord, or else we're all gonna die?<br /><br />That pretty much sums up the theme of the main epic.<br /><br />I am a DEVOTED lover of all things prog-rock, going back to Yes, Genesis, Pink Floyd and Emerson, Lake & Palmer - yes, I'm that old.  And I simply worship the music of The Flower Kings, IQ, Kaipa, Riverside, Porcupine Tree, The Tangent, etc. etc., so I am no stranger to the \\"new\\" prog-rock scene.  It is amazing this music lives on, and gets better and better with age.  I also LOVED the past Transatlantic records, and just about anything Roine Stolt does!<br /><br />Yet it is the MESSAGE here that bothers me.  I don't mind spirituality - in fact, it is obvious much of The Flower Kings and previous Transatlantic records, especially Bridge Across Forever, speaks to the subject of our spiritual being.  But usually it is presented as more of a life-style, a way to be, a kinder, gentler way of living to be in sync with nature and the planet (i.e. \\"There Is More To This World\\").  Or it makes us think, \\"What If\\" (i.e. \\"Bridge Across Forever\\" - \\"...my love, she is standing by the side, of a Magic River, that there is crossing.  Maybe someday when our spirits begin to rise, and light fills our eyes... We will meet again...\\")  It is hopeful and makes us feel good to believe there MAY be a \\"forever\\" where we will meet those we love who passed before us.  This is beautiful stuff (listening to it now)!  I'm an agnostic, so Ibelieve we have no idea what eternity holds for us!  Much of this music speaks to that yearning for the unknown.<br /><br />But this has devolved into the standard, Bible-thumping, \\"man, you better get on your knees and worship the Lord or else we are all GONNA DIE!!\\"  I dislike and disapprove of the Christian philosophy that the \\"giver of life\\", God, is such an arrogant jerk to require you to bend on knee and succumb to him, or else you will burn in eternal hell.  That sure has never sounded very nice to me, for somebody as all powerful and all forgiving as God!  And, unfortunately, that's the message here.<br /><br />Plus, don't be fooled by some reviewers here, EVERY song has SOME religious imagery, all leading to that idea that we are in trouble if we don't serve the Lord.<br /><br />Personally, I think the world is in trouble because too many different people have too many different ideas about what \\"serving the Lord\\" is!  It causes chaos, not calm.<br /><br />So, Neal, you're a great musician, but your religion has turned too much into the Holy Roller brand, and you've lost me.  And please, Neal, please quit being like the conservative religious hypocrites Roine sometimes reflects on in HIS songs, by doing everything you can to FORCE me to listen your lectures!<br /><br />Whew, having said that, and now that I've turned off all the religious fans of this band, I must say the THREE stars are for musical excellence!  This IS great music!  Okay, even musically, I don't think quite as grand or flowing as the great \\"Duel With the Devil\\" from BAF.  It's odd for me to say this (as somebody who enjoys TFK's Garden of Dreams 60-minute opus), but this is too long!  Some parts drag.  I think this could have been cut about 20 minutes and been better.  All that \\"the giver of life is asking you to DANCE...\\" dance dance dance -the \\"dance\\" bit goes on a bit long - geez, Neal, if I wanted to listen to a dance record I would have put on some hip-hop!  I don't even like dancing!(\\"We Don't Dance\\"...:))<br /><br />Musical highlights here must include Portnoy's drumming!  Man, there are some mean drum licks in here!  And some wicked syncronized playing, drums, guitar, bass and keys!  But overall, it falls a bit flat for me.<br /><br />Why am I complaining?  I don't like that a) this was not released as an MP3 so I could hear it before plopping down the big bucks to get the CD, and b) there were NO samples to listen to.  I bought this on the strength of their previous albums, and I'm a bit disappointed.  Plus, I was not aware this was blatant, preachy Christian rock; see complaint about samples below.<br /><br />Is it terrible?  No.  But is it a leap forward from their past work?  No.  Anything you hear on this album, you've heard on the others.<br /><br />I just listened to it in my car one more time, blaring loud yesterday, and I'm convinced this won't earn a regular rotation on my list.  It's just not THAT good, as it should be.<br /><br />I would suggest, as alternatives, the VERY impressive album by Jonas Reingold's Karmakanic, \\"Who's the Boss In the Factory?\\", or the amazing IQ album \\"Frequency\\", or how about Unifaun's debut album!  \\"Who's the Boss...\\" is especially fantastic.  The closing song, \\"Eternally Part 2\\" may be one of the most dramatic prog songs ever recorded!  It's simply beautiful and yes, it DOES touch on the subject of the prospect of eternal life (\\"I fear eternity is near, I give myself to you, eternally\\").  It's less preachy, leaving your own interpretation of what IS eternity going to be?<br /><br />If you've read this far, maybe you're like I was: doubtful about the religious focus of this album.  I want to confirm: yes, this is VERY religous, and it interferes with the music, which itself just isn't as top notch as you would expect from these top notch players.
0141439807|4.0|Even the best authors in the world sometimes put out something that... well, isn't up to their usual standards. For Jane Austen, that book was \\"Mansfield Park\\" -- her prose is typically excellent, and she weaves a memorable story about a poor young lady in the middle of a wealthy, dysfunctional family. But put bluntly, Fanny Price lacks the depth and complexity of Austen's other heroines.<br /><br />As a young girl, Fanny Price was sent from her poor family to live with her wealth relatives, the Bertrams, and was raised along with her four cousins Tom, Edmund, Maria and Julia.<br /><br />Despite being regarded only little better than a servant (especially by the fawning, cheap Mrs. Norris), Fanny is pretty happy -- especially since Edmund is kind and supportive of her at all times. But then the charming, fashionable Crawford sibilings arrive in the neighborhood, sparking off some love triangles (particularly between Maria and Henry Crawford, even though she's already engaged.<br /><br />And the whole thing becomes even more confused when Henry becomes intrigued by Fanny's refusal to be charmed by him as the others are. But when she rejects his proposal, she ends up banished from her beloved Mansfield Park... right before a devastating scandal and a perilous illness strikes the Bertram family. Does Fanny still have a chance at love and the family she's always been with?<br /><br />The biggest problem with \\"Mansfield Park\\" is Fanny Price -- even Austen's own mother didn't like her. She's a very flat, virtuously dull heroine for this story; unlike Austen's other heroines she doesn't have much personality growth or a personal flaw to overcome. And despite being the protagonist, Fanny seems more like a spectator on the outskirts of the plot until the second half (when she has a small but pivotal part to play in the story).<br /><br />Fortunately she's the only real flaw in this book. Austen's stately, vivid prose is full of deliciously witty moments (Aunt Norris \\"consoled herself for the loss of her husband by considering that she could do very well without him\\"), some tastefully-handled scandal, and a delicate house-of-romantic-cards that comes crashing down to ruin people's lives (and improve others). And she inserts some pointed commentary on people who care more about society's opinions than on morality.<br /><br />And the other characters in the book are pretty fascinating as well -- especially since Edmund, despite being a virtuous clergyman-in-training, is an intelligent and strong-willed man. The Bertrams are a rather dysfunctional family with a stern patriarch, a fluttery ethereal mother, a playboy heir and a couple of spoiled girls -- Maria in particular develops a crush on Henry, but doesn't bother to break off her engagement until it's too late. And the Crawfords are all flash and sparkle: a pair of charming, shallow people who are essentially hollow.<br /><br />\\"Mansfield Park\\" suffers from a rather insipid heroine, but the rest of the book is vintage Austen -- lies, romance, scandal and a dance of manners and society.d herself for the loss of her husband by considering that she could do very well without him\\"), some tastefully-handled scandal, and a delicate house-of-romantic-cards that comes crashing down to ruin people's lives (and improve others). B002VPE1B6|4.0|First, this is just the Blu Ray disk with no extra features. It was fine for me because I bought the DVD because I wasn't able to see the movie in the theaters and so wanted to at least see it on my wide screen TV at home.<br /><br />I have a decent, medium-price range Yamaha upscaling video and audio processor and the movie looked great at 1080p on my three year old Samsung LCD TV using a modestly priced Panasonic Blu Ray player. I know people who prefer plasma TVs because they say the color quality is somewhat better (I wouldn't know as I'm not an expert here), but for me it looked great as is.<br /><br />As for the movie, I really enjoyed the spectacular visuals and CGI effects, and it's the movie's real strength. The world of Pandora is just beautifully and fully realized, with fantastic ecology and sci-fi ideas that could have been pulled from at least a dozen sci-fi and fantasy authors that I can think of. Too bad Robert Heinlein didn't live long enough to see all these movies with people in mechanized armor which he wrote about in his book, Starship Troopers, back in 1959, now a half a century ago. And I think Anne McCaffrey (Dragon Riders of Pern, etc.) would like the dragons and how their relationship with the Na'vi are portrayed. James Schmitz pioneered writing ecologically oriented science fiction, and of course there's Frank Herbert's famous Dune novel and series. This movie has elements from all of those great writers.<br /><br />But as for the story line, it's fine, but there's nothing especially original here, and the movie is sort of like Pocahontas meets Duke Nukum. The humans are the bad guys trying to mine a highly valuable mineral, but they have to get rid of the natives first. Oddly enough, this was the same plot of another recent animated flick, the name escapes me unfortunately, but humans were trying to terraform their world and take it for themselves. This seems to be a popular plot idea these days.<br /><br />Some of the details of the ecology were original, such as the idea that the giant trees form a huge, planet-wide communications network, sort of like a neural network or gigantic brain. And that the natives could tap into the knowledge stored there. That was cool.<br /><br />I saw a TV show recently that showed how they used mini-cams to film the actors' faces so as to make the expressions more realistic. This process seemed to work out very well, too.<br /><br />In summary, it's a movie with great special effects, with lots of fun ideas pulled from a lot of the great science fiction and fantasy writers of the past to create a very cool planetary ecology, a sympathetic alien species who just want to be left alone in their little paradise, and greedy human bad guys who don't mind killing the natives if they can make a profit. Overall, a truly spectacular movie visually even if there wasn't anything especially original about the story. I give it a solid four stars since it's really a three star movie plot/idea with five star special effects.dea that the giant trees form a huge, planet-wide communications network, sort of like a neural network or gigantic brain. 030745455X|3.0|I think the popularity of this series of books relates to Lisabeth's one-sided \\"answer\\" to a universal ethical conundrum related to boundaries, namely, ought we respect the personal boundaries of other people if we suspect they may be up to no good?  Lisabeth uses her computer hacking skills to get inside of the minds and lives of virtually every other character in the novel, and she implicitly justifies her modus operandi by doing it all in the pursuit of social justice.  Each of us as individuals, as well as organizations up to the level of sovereign nations, routinely faces the same temptation as we frantically try to control the directions in which our worlds are headed.  If we suspect our kids are doing drugs, should we not snoop in their private diaries?  If we think their friends are bad influences, should we not install spying and monitoring equipment on their computers?  Lisabeth Salendar provides the answer at one end of the spectrum, but this novel fails to make any case at all for what it costs us in terms of trust to condone such wholesale boundary violations, both as individuals and as a society.
